<table cellspacing="0" cellpadding="0" style="background:transparent; width:100%;">
<tbody><tr>
<td style="width:50%; text-align:left;"><b>President before election</b><br />
<p><a href="/wiki/Dwight_D._Eisenhower" title="Dwight D. Eisenhower">Dwight D. Eisenhower</a><br />
<a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Republican Party (United States)">Republican</a>
</p>
</td>
<td style="width:50%; text-align:right;"><b>Elected President</b> <br />
<p><a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">John F. Kennedy</a><br />
<a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="Democratic Party (United States)">Democratic</a>
</p>
</td></tr></tbody></table></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The <b>1960 United States presidential election</b> was the 44th quadrennial <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election" title="United States presidential election">presidential election</a>. It was held on Tuesday, November 8, 1960. In a closely contested election, <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="Democratic Party (United States)">Democrat</a> <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">John F. Kennedy</a> defeated incumbent <a href="/wiki/Vice_President_of_the_United_States" title="Vice President of the United States">Vice President</a> <a href="/wiki/Richard_Nixon" title="Richard Nixon">Richard Nixon</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Republican Party (United States)">Republican Party</a> nominee. This was the first election in which all fifty states participated, and the last in which the <a href="/wiki/District_of_Columbia" class="mw-redirect" title="District of Columbia">District of Columbia</a> did not. It was also the first election in which an incumbent president was ineligible to run for a third term due to the term limits established by the <a href="/wiki/Twenty-second_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Twenty-second Amendment to the United States Constitution">22nd Amendment</a>. It is also the last election where the losing candidate won <a href="/wiki/Ohio" title="Ohio">Ohio</a>.
</p><p>Nixon faced little opposition in the Republican race to succeed popular incumbent <a href="/wiki/Dwight_D._Eisenhower" title="Dwight D. Eisenhower">Dwight D. Eisenhower</a>. Kennedy, a <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate" title="United States Senate">U.S. Senator</a> from <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts" title="Massachusetts">Massachusetts</a>, established himself as the Democratic front-runner with his strong performance in the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_presidential_primaries,_1960" class="mw-redirect" title="Democratic Party presidential primaries, 1960">1960 Democratic primaries</a>, including a key victory in <a href="/wiki/West_Virginia" title="West Virginia">West Virginia</a> over Senator <a href="/wiki/Hubert_Humphrey" title="Hubert Humphrey">Hubert Humphrey</a>. He defeated Senate Majority Leader <a href="/wiki/Lyndon_B._Johnson" title="Lyndon B. Johnson">Lyndon B. Johnson</a> on the first presidential ballot of the <a href="/wiki/1960_Democratic_National_Convention" title="1960 Democratic National Convention">1960 Democratic National Convention</a>, and asked Johnson to serve as his running mate. The issue of the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a> dominated the election, as tensions were high between the United States and the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>.
</p><p>Kennedy won a 303 to 219 <a href="/wiki/Electoral_College_(United_States)" class="mw-redirect" title="Electoral College (United States)">Electoral College</a> victory, and is generally considered to have won the national popular vote by 112,827, a margin of 0.17 percent. The issue of the popular vote was complicated by the presence of several <a href="/wiki/Unpledged_elector" title="Unpledged elector">unpledged electors</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Deep_South" title="Deep South">Deep South</a>. Fourteen unpledged electors from <a href="/wiki/Mississippi" title="Mississippi">Mississippi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alabama" title="Alabama">Alabama</a> cast their vote for Senator <a href="/wiki/Harry_F._Byrd" title="Harry F. Byrd">Harry F. Byrd</a>, as did a <a href="/wiki/Faithless_elector" title="Faithless elector">faithless elector</a> from <a href="/wiki/Oklahoma" title="Oklahoma">Oklahoma</a>. The 1960 presidential election was the closest election since <a href="/wiki/1916_United_States_presidential_election" title="1916 United States presidential election">1916</a>, and this closeness can be explained by a number of factors.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Kennedy benefited from the <a href="/wiki/Recession_of_1958" title="Recession of 1958">economic recession of 1957–58</a>, which hurt the standing of the <a href="/wiki/Incumbent" title="Incumbent">incumbent</a> Republican Party, and he had the advantage of 17 million more registered Democrats than Republicans.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Furthermore, the new votes that Kennedy, the first <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholic">Roman Catholic</a> president, gained among Catholics almost neutralized the new votes Nixon gained among <a href="/wiki/Protestantism_in_the_United_States" title="Protestantism in the United States">Protestants</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Kennedy's campaigning skills decisively outmatched Nixon's, who wasted time and resources campaigning in all fifty states while Kennedy focused on campaigning in populous <a href="/wiki/Swing_states" class="mw-redirect" title="Swing states">swing states</a>. Nixon's emphasis on his experience carried little weight for most voters. Kennedy used his large, well-funded campaign organization to win the nomination, secure endorsements, and, with the aid of the <a href="/wiki/Political_boss" title="Political boss">big-city bosses</a>, get out the vote in the big cities.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Kennedy relied on Johnson to hold the South, and used television effectively.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> In 1963, Kennedy was <a href="/wiki/Assassination_of_John_F._Kennedy" title="Assassination of John F. Kennedy">assassinated</a> and was succeeded by Johnson. Nixon would later successfully seek the presidency in <a href="/wiki/1968_United_States_presidential_election" title="1968 United States presidential election">1968</a>.
</p>

<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Democratic_candidates">Democratic candidates</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=1960_United_States_presidential_election&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Democratic candidates">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional" style="max-width: 978px;_width: 978px;">
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px"><div style="width: 155px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:John_F._Kennedy_-_NARA_-_518134.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/John_F._Kennedy_-_NARA_-_518134.jpg/94px-John_F._Kennedy_-_NARA_-_518134.jpg" decoding="async" width="94" height="120" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/John_F._Kennedy_-_NARA_-_518134.jpg/141px-John_F._Kennedy_-_NARA_-_518134.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/John_F._Kennedy_-_NARA_-_518134.jpg/188px-John_F._Kennedy_-_NARA_-_518134.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2353" data-file-height="3000" /></a></div></div>

			<div class="gallerytext">
<p>Former <a href="/wiki/Governor_of_Illinois" title="Governor of Illinois">Governor</a> <b><a href="/wiki/Adlai_Stevenson_II" title="Adlai Stevenson II">Adlai Stevenson</a></b> of <a href="/wiki/Illinois" title="Illinois">Illinois</a>
</p>
			</div>
		</div></li>
</ul>
<p>The major candidates for the 1960 Democratic presidential nomination were <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">John F. Kennedy</a>, <a href="/wiki/California_Governor" class="mw-redirect" title="California Governor">Governor</a> <a href="/wiki/Pat_Brown" title="Pat Brown">Pat Brown</a> of California, Senator <a href="/wiki/Stuart_Symington" title="Stuart Symington">Stuart Symington</a> from <a href="/wiki/Missouri" title="Missouri">Missouri</a>, Senator <a href="/wiki/Lyndon_B._Johnson" title="Lyndon B. Johnson">Lyndon B. Johnson</a> from Texas, former Illinois Governor <a href="/wiki/Adlai_Stevenson_II" title="Adlai Stevenson II">Adlai Stevenson</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Senator <a href="/wiki/Wayne_Morse" title="Wayne Morse">Wayne Morse</a> from <a href="/wiki/Oregon" title="Oregon">Oregon</a>, and Senator <a href="/wiki/Hubert_Humphrey" title="Hubert Humphrey">Hubert Humphrey</a> from <a href="/wiki/Minnesota" title="Minnesota">Minnesota</a>. Several other candidates sought support in their home state or region as "<a href="/wiki/Favorite_son" title="Favorite son">favorite son</a>" candidates without any realistic chance of winning the nomination. Symington, Stevenson, and Johnson all declined to campaign in the presidential primaries. While this reduced their potential delegate count going into the Democratic National Convention, each of these three candidates hoped that the other leading contenders would stumble in the primaries, thus causing the convention's delegates to choose him as a "compromise" candidate acceptable to all factions of the party.
</p><p>Kennedy was initially dogged by suggestions from some Democratic Party elders (such as former President <a href="/wiki/Harry_S._Truman" title="Harry S. Truman">Harry S. Truman</a>, who was supporting Symington) that he was too youthful and inexperienced to be president; these critics suggested that he should agree to be the <a href="/wiki/Running_mate" title="Running mate">running mate</a> for another Democrat. Realizing that this was a strategy touted by his opponents to keep the public from taking him seriously, Kennedy stated frankly, "I'm not running for vice president, I'm running for president."<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1960_Dem_Primaries.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/1960_Dem_Primaries.svg/400px-1960_Dem_Primaries.svg.png" decoding="async" width="400" height="233" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/1960_Dem_Primaries.svg/600px-1960_Dem_Primaries.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/1960_Dem_Primaries.svg/800px-1960_Dem_Primaries.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1020" data-file-height="593" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1960_Dem_Primaries.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>1960 Democratic primaries results</div></div></div>
<p>The next step was the primaries. Kennedy's <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Roman Catholic</a> religion was an issue. Kennedy first challenged Minnesota Senator Hubert Humphrey in the Wisconsin primary and defeated him. Kennedy's attractive sisters, brothers, and wife Jacqueline combed the state looking for votes, leading Humphrey to complain that he "felt like an independent merchant competing against a chain store."<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> However, some political experts argued that Kennedy's margin of victory had come almost entirely from Catholic areas, and thus Humphrey decided to continue the contest in the heavily Protestant state of West Virginia. The first televised debate of 1960 was held in West Virginia, and Kennedy outperformed Humphrey.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Humphrey's campaign was low on funds and could not compete for advertising and other "get-out-the-vote" drives with Kennedy's well-financed and well-organized campaign. In the end, Kennedy defeated Humphrey with over 60% of the vote, and Humphrey ended his presidential campaign. <a href="/wiki/West_Virginia" title="West Virginia">West Virginia</a> showed that Kennedy, a Catholic, could win in a heavily Protestant state. Although Kennedy had only competed in nine presidential primaries,<sup id="cite_ref-N&amp;O_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-N&amp;O-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Kennedy's rivals, Johnson and Symington, failed to campaign in any primaries. Even though Stevenson had twice been the Democratic Party's presidential candidate and retained a loyal following of liberals, his two landslide defeats to Republican <a href="/wiki/Dwight_Eisenhower" class="mw-redirect" title="Dwight Eisenhower">Dwight Eisenhower</a> led most party leaders and delegates to search for a "fresh face" who could win a national election. Following the primaries, Kennedy traveled around the nation speaking to state delegations and their leaders. As the Democratic Convention opened, Kennedy was far in the lead, but was still seen as being just short of the delegate total he needed to win.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Democratic_convention">Democratic convention</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=1960_United_States_presidential_election&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Democratic convention">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/1960_Democratic_National_Convention" title="1960 Democratic National Convention">1960 Democratic National Convention</a> was held in <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles" title="Los Angeles">Los Angeles, California</a>. In the week before the convention opened, Kennedy received two new challengers when Lyndon B. Johnson, the powerful <a href="/wiki/Party_leaders_of_the_United_States_Senate" title="Party leaders of the United States Senate">Senate Majority Leader</a> from Texas, and Adlai Stevenson, the party's nominee in 1952 and 1956, officially announced their candidacies (they had both privately been working for the nomination for some time). However, neither Johnson nor Stevenson was a match for the talented and highly efficient Kennedy campaign team led by <a href="/wiki/Robert_F._Kennedy" title="Robert F. Kennedy">Robert F. Kennedy</a>. Johnson challenged Kennedy to a televised debate before a joint meeting of the Texas and Massachusetts delegations, to which Kennedy accepted. Most observers believed that Kennedy won the debate, and Johnson was unable to expand his delegate support beyond the South. Stevenson's failure to launch his candidacy publicly until the week of the convention meant that many liberal delegates who might have supported him were already pledged to Kennedy, and Stevenson—despite the energetic support of former First Lady <a href="/wiki/Eleanor_Roosevelt" title="Eleanor Roosevelt">Eleanor Roosevelt</a>—could not break their allegiance. Kennedy won the nomination on the first ballot.
</p><p>Then, in a move that surprised many, Kennedy asked Johnson to be his running mate. He realized that he could not be elected without the support of traditional <a href="/wiki/Southern_Democrats" title="Southern Democrats">Southern Democrats</a>, most of whom had backed Johnson. He offered Johnson the vice presidential nomination at the <a href="/wiki/Millennium_Biltmore_Hotel" title="Millennium Biltmore Hotel">Los Angeles Biltmore Hotel</a> at 10:15&#160;a.m. on July 14, 1960, the morning after being nominated for president.<sup id="cite_ref-Caro_2012,_pp._121–135._JFK_offers_vice_presidency_to_LBJ_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Caro_2012,_pp._121–135._JFK_offers_vice_presidency_to_LBJ-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Robert_F._Kennedy" title="Robert F. Kennedy">Robert F. Kennedy</a>, who hated Johnson for his attacks on the Kennedy family, and who favored labor leader <a href="/wiki/Walter_Reuther" title="Walter Reuther">Walter Reuther</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> later said that his brother offered the position to Johnson as a courtesy and did not predict him to accept it. <a href="/wiki/Arthur_M._Schlesinger_Jr." title="Arthur M. Schlesinger Jr.">Arthur M. Schlesinger Jr.</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Seymour_Hersh" title="Seymour Hersh">Seymour Hersh</a> quoted Robert Kennedy's version of events, writing that John Kennedy "would have preferred <a href="/wiki/Stuart_Symington" title="Stuart Symington">Stuart Symington</a> as his <a href="/wiki/Running-mate" class="mw-redirect" title="Running-mate">running-mate</a>" and that Johnson teamed with <a href="/wiki/Speaker_of_the_U.S._House_of_Representatives" class="mw-redirect" title="Speaker of the U.S. House of Representatives">House Speaker</a> <a href="/wiki/Sam_Rayburn" title="Sam Rayburn">Sam Rayburn</a> to "pressure Kennedy to offer the nomination". Hersh goes on to present an alternative version of events; he writes that Kennedy was essentially blackmailed into offering the vice presidency to Johnson. The same story was originally presented by <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Summers" title="Anthony Summers">Anthony Summers</a> in his biography of FBI Director <a href="/wiki/J._Edgar_Hoover" title="J. Edgar Hoover">J. Edgar Hoover</a>. <a href="/wiki/Evelyn_Lincoln" title="Evelyn Lincoln">Evelyn Lincoln</a>, Kennedy's personal secretary, told Summers in an interview that she was convinced in mid-1960 that J. Edgar Hoover and Johnson had conspired. Hoover was known to keep detailed files on the personal lives of many political figures and Kennedy was no exception. Hoover obtained information about Jack's womanizing prior to the election from at least two different sources. In January 1942, while Jack was serving in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy" title="United States Navy">United States Navy</a>, FBI surveillance records confirmed that he was having an affair with a woman named <a href="/wiki/Inga_Arvad" title="Inga Arvad">Inga Arvad</a>; then, in 1958, a couple named Leonard and Florence Kater found out that their tenant, <a href="/wiki/Pamela_Turnure" title="Pamela Turnure">Pamela Turnure</a>, a secretary in Kennedy's Senate office, had been having an affair with the soon-to-be president. The Katers rigged up a tape recorder to pick up the sounds of the couple's lovemaking and snapped a picture of Kennedy himself. The Katers sent this information to the newspapers and one company - Stearn Publications - passed it along to Hoover. Soon after, "he quietly obtained a copy of the compromising sex tapes and offered them to Lyndon Johnson as campaign ammunition." Lincoln said that Johnson "had been using all the information Hoover could find on Kennedy - during the campaign, even before the Convention. And Hoover was in on the pressure on Kennedy at the Convention." A few days after the offer was made, <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Salinger" title="Pierre Salinger">Pierre Salinger</a>, the campaign's press secretary, had asked Jack Kennedy whether he really expected Johnson to accept the offer or if he was merely making a polite gesture. Kennedy responded cryptically: "The whole story will never be known. And it's just as well that it won't be." "The only people who were involved in the discussions were Jack and myself," said Robert Kennedy. "We both promised each other that we'd never tell what happened." <sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Biographers <a href="/wiki/Robert_Caro" title="Robert Caro">Robert Caro</a> and <a href="/wiki/W._Marvin_Watson" title="W. Marvin Watson">W. Marvin Watson</a> offer a different perspective; they write that the Kennedy campaign was desperate to win what was forecast to be a very close race against <a href="/wiki/Richard_Nixon" title="Richard Nixon">Richard Nixon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Henry_Cabot_Lodge_Jr." title="Henry Cabot Lodge Jr.">Henry Cabot Lodge Jr.</a>. Johnson was needed on the ticket to help carry votes from Texas and the <a href="/wiki/Southern_United_States" title="Southern United States">Southern United States</a>. Caro's research showed that on July 14, Kennedy started the process while Johnson was still asleep. At 6:30&#160;a.m., Kennedy asked his brother to prepare an estimate of upcoming electoral votes, "including Texas."<sup id="cite_ref-Caro_2012,_pp._121–135._JFK_offers_vice_presidency_to_LBJ_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Caro_2012,_pp._121–135._JFK_offers_vice_presidency_to_LBJ-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Robert Kennedy called <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Salinger" title="Pierre Salinger">Pierre Salinger</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kenneth_O%27Donnell" title="Kenneth O&#39;Donnell">Kenneth O'Donnell</a> to assist him. Realizing the ramifications of counting Texas votes as their own, Salinger asked him whether he was considering a Kennedy-Johnson ticket, and Robert replied, "yes".<sup id="cite_ref-Caro_2012,_pp._121–135._JFK_offers_vice_presidency_to_LBJ_12-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Caro_2012,_pp._121–135._JFK_offers_vice_presidency_to_LBJ-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Between 9 and 10 a.m., John Kennedy called Pennsylvania governor <a href="/wiki/David_L._Lawrence" title="David L. Lawrence">David L. Lawrence</a>, a Johnson backer, to request that Lawrence nominate Johnson for vice president if Johnson were to accept the role and then went to Johnson's suite to discuss a mutual ticket at 10:15&#160;a.m. John Kennedy then returned to his suite to announce the Kennedy-Johnson ticket to his closest supporters and Northern political bosses. He accepted the congratulations of Ohio governor <a href="/wiki/Michael_DiSalle" title="Michael DiSalle">Michael DiSalle</a>, Connecticut governor <a href="/wiki/Abraham_A._Ribicoff" class="mw-redirect" title="Abraham A. Ribicoff">Abraham A. Ribicoff</a>, Chicago mayor <a href="/wiki/Richard_J._Daley" title="Richard J. Daley">Richard J. Daley</a>, and New York City mayor <a href="/wiki/Robert_F._Wagner_Jr." title="Robert F. Wagner Jr.">Robert F. Wagner Jr.</a>. Lawrence said that "Johnson has the strength where you need it most"; he then left to begin writing the nomination speech.<sup id="cite_ref-Caro_2012,_pp._121–135._JFK_offers_vice_presidency_to_LBJ_12-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Caro_2012,_pp._121–135._JFK_offers_vice_presidency_to_LBJ-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> O'Donnell remembers being angry at what he considered a betrayal by John Kennedy, who had previously cast Johnson as anti-labor and anti-liberal. Afterward, Robert Kennedy visited with labor leaders who were extremely unhappy with the choice of Johnson and after seeing the depth of labor opposition to Johnson, he ran messages between the hotel suites of his brother and Johnson, apparently trying to undermine the proposed ticket without John Kennedy's authorization and to get Johnson to agree to be the Democratic Party chairman rather than vice president. Johnson refused to accept a change in plans unless it came directly from John Kennedy. Despite his brother's interference, John Kennedy was firm that Johnson was who he wanted as running mate and met with staffers such as <a href="/wiki/Larry_O%27Brien" title="Larry O&#39;Brien">Larry O'Brien</a>, his national campaign manager, to say Johnson was to be vice president. O'Brien recalled later that John Kennedy's words were wholly unexpected, but that after a brief consideration of the electoral vote situation, he thought "it was a stroke of genius".<sup id="cite_ref-Caro_2012,_pp._121–135._JFK_offers_vice_presidency_to_LBJ_12-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Caro_2012,_pp._121–135._JFK_offers_vice_presidency_to_LBJ-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Norman_Mailer" title="Norman Mailer">Norman Mailer</a> attended the convention and wrote his famous profile of Kennedy, "Superman Comes to the Supermart," published in <i><a href="/wiki/Esquire_(magazine)" title="Esquire (magazine)">Esquire</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-la_times_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-la_times-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Republican_Party">Republican Party</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=1960_United_States_presidential_election&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Republican Party">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/1960_Republican_Party_presidential_primaries" title="1960 Republican Party presidential primaries">1960 Republican Party presidential primaries</a></div>
<table class="wikitable" style="font-size:90%; text-align:center;">

<tbody><tr>
<td style="background:#f1f1f1;" colspan="30"><big><b>Republican Party ticket, 1960</b></big>
</td></tr>
<tr>

<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Republican_candidates">Republican candidates</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=1960_United_States_presidential_election&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Republican candidates">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional" style="max-width: 978px;_width: 978px;">
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px"><div style="width: 155px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:VP-Nixon_copy.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/VP-Nixon_copy.jpg/101px-VP-Nixon_copy.jpg" decoding="async" width="101" height="120" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/VP-Nixon_copy.jpg/151px-VP-Nixon_copy.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/VP-Nixon_copy.jpg/201px-VP-Nixon_copy.jpg 2x" data-file-width="416" data-file-height="496" /></a></div></div>

</p>

		</div></li>

</p>
			</div>
		</div></li>
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px"><div style="width: 155px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cecil_H._Underwood.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Cecil_H._Underwood.jpg/91px-Cecil_H._Underwood.jpg" decoding="async" width="91" height="120" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Cecil_H._Underwood.jpg/137px-Cecil_H._Underwood.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Cecil_H._Underwood.jpg/183px-Cecil_H._Underwood.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1021" data-file-height="1340" /></a></div></div>
			<div class="gallerytext">
<p>Governor <b><a href="/wiki/Cecil_H._Underwood" title="Cecil H. Underwood">Cecil H. Underwood</a></b> of <a href="/wiki/West_Virginia" title="West Virginia">West Virginia</a>
</p>
			</div>
		</div></li>
</ul>
<p>With the ratification of the <a href="/wiki/Twenty-second_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Twenty-second Amendment to the United States Constitution">22nd Amendment</a> in 1951, President Dwight D. Eisenhower could not run for the office of president again; he had been elected in 1952 and 1956.
</p><p>In 1959, it looked as if Vice President <a href="/wiki/Richard_Nixon" title="Richard Nixon">Richard Nixon</a> might face a serious challenge for the <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Republican Party (United States)">Republican</a> nomination from New York Governor <a href="/wiki/Nelson_Rockefeller" title="Nelson Rockefeller">Nelson Rockefeller</a>, the leader of the Republican moderate-liberal wing. However, Rockefeller announced that he would not be a candidate for president after a national tour revealed that the great majority of Republicans favored Nixon.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>After Rockefeller's withdrawal, Nixon faced no significant opposition for the Republican nomination. At the <a href="/wiki/1960_Republican_National_Convention" title="1960 Republican National Convention">1960 Republican National Convention</a> in <a href="/wiki/Chicago" title="Chicago">Chicago, Illinois</a>, Nixon was the overwhelming choice of the delegates, with conservative Senator <a href="/wiki/Barry_Goldwater" title="Barry Goldwater">Barry Goldwater</a> from Arizona receiving 10 votes from conservative delegates. In earning the nomination, Nixon became the first sitting vice president to run for president since <a href="/wiki/John_C._Breckinridge" title="John C. Breckinridge">John C. Breckinridge</a> exactly a century prior. Nixon then chose former Massachusetts Senator and <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> Ambassador <a href="/wiki/Henry_Cabot_Lodge_Jr." title="Henry Cabot Lodge Jr.">Henry Cabot Lodge Jr.</a>, as his vice presidential running mate. Nixon chose Lodge because his foreign-policy credentials fit into Nixon's strategy to campaign more on foreign policy than domestic policy, which he believed favored the Democrats. Nixon had previously sought Rockefeller as his running mate, but the governor had no ambitions to be vice president. However, he later served as <a href="/wiki/Gerald_Ford" title="Gerald Ford">Gerald Ford</a>'s vice president from 1974 to 1977.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="General_election">General election</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=1960_United_States_presidential_election&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: General election">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Campaign_promises">Campaign promises</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=1960_United_States_presidential_election&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Campaign promises">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dwight_David_Eisenhower,_photo_portrait_by_Bachrach,_1952.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Dwight_David_Eisenhower%2C_photo_portrait_by_Bachrach%2C_1952.jpg/170px-Dwight_David_Eisenhower%2C_photo_portrait_by_Bachrach%2C_1952.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="211" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Dwight_David_Eisenhower%2C_photo_portrait_by_Bachrach%2C_1952.jpg/255px-Dwight_David_Eisenhower%2C_photo_portrait_by_Bachrach%2C_1952.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Dwight_David_Eisenhower%2C_photo_portrait_by_Bachrach%2C_1952.jpg/340px-Dwight_David_Eisenhower%2C_photo_portrait_by_Bachrach%2C_1952.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2863" data-file-height="3558" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dwight_David_Eisenhower,_photo_portrait_by_Bachrach,_1952.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Dwight_D._Eisenhower" title="Dwight D. Eisenhower">Dwight D. Eisenhower</a>, the incumbent president in 1960, whose term expired on January 20, 1961</div></div></div>
<p>During the campaign, Kennedy charged that under Eisenhower and the Republicans the nation had fallen behind the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a>, both militarily and economically, and that as president he would "get America moving again." The Eisenhower administration had established NASA in 1958, but Kennedy believed that the Republican Party had ignored the need to catch up to the Soviet Union in the Space Race. He promised that the new Democratic administration would fully appreciate the importance of space accomplishments for the national security and international prestige of the United States. Nixon responded that, if elected, he would continue the "peace and prosperity" that Eisenhower had brought the nation in the 1950s.  Nixon also argued that with the nation engaged in the Cold War with the Soviets, that Kennedy was too young and inexperienced to be trusted with the presidency. Had Nixon been elected, at 48 years, 11 days, he would have been <a href="/wiki/List_of_Presidents_of_the_United_States_by_age" class="mw-redirect" title="List of Presidents of the United States by age">the fourth-youngest</a> president at the date of inauguration. Kennedy, by contrast, was 43 years, 236 days, on the date of his inauguration; the second-youngest man to begin a Presidency (at 42 <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Roosevelt" title="Theodore Roosevelt">Theodore Roosevelt</a>, who assumed the Presidency upon the assassination of President <a href="/wiki/William_McKinley" title="William McKinley">William McKinley</a> 60 years previously, was the youngest).
</p><p>During Kennedy's campaign he relied on his youth and promised to bring about change. Kennedy had a slogan emphasizing his youth reading, "who's seasoned through and through/but not so dog-gone seasoned that he won't try something new." He was also endorsed by celebrities such as <a href="/wiki/Henry_Fonda" title="Henry Fonda">Henry Fonda</a> and <a href="/wiki/Harry_Belafonte" title="Harry Belafonte">Harry Belafonte</a>. Nixon relied on his experience in politics. He emphasized to the American voters that he was more qualified to hold the office of president. He wanted voters to know that he had the abilities to take on Communist threats.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Campaign_events">Campaign events</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=1960_United_States_presidential_election&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Campaign events">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:John_F._Kennedy_campaigns_in_LaGrange,_Georgia_1960.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/John_F._Kennedy_campaigns_in_LaGrange%2C_Georgia_1960.jpg/220px-John_F._Kennedy_campaigns_in_LaGrange%2C_Georgia_1960.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="282" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/John_F._Kennedy_campaigns_in_LaGrange%2C_Georgia_1960.jpg/330px-John_F._Kennedy_campaigns_in_LaGrange%2C_Georgia_1960.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/John_F._Kennedy_campaigns_in_LaGrange%2C_Georgia_1960.jpg/440px-John_F._Kennedy_campaigns_in_LaGrange%2C_Georgia_1960.jpg 2x" data-file-width="980" data-file-height="1254" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:John_F._Kennedy_campaigns_in_LaGrange,_Georgia_1960.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Kennedy campaigning in <a href="/wiki/LaGrange,_Georgia" title="LaGrange, Georgia">LaGrange, Georgia</a>, October 1960</div></div></div>
<p>Kennedy and Nixon both drew large and enthusiastic crowds throughout the campaign.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> In August 1960, most polls gave Nixon a slim lead over Kennedy, and many political <a href="/wiki/Pundit_(expert)" class="mw-redirect" title="Pundit (expert)">pundits</a> regarded him as the favorite to win. However, Nixon was plagued by bad luck throughout the fall campaign. In August, President Eisenhower, who had long been ambivalent about Nixon, held a televised press conference in which a reporter, Charles Mohr of <i><a href="/wiki/Time_(magazine)" title="Time (magazine)">Time</a></i>, mentioned Nixon's claims that he had been a valuable administration insider and adviser. Mohr asked Eisenhower if he could give an example of a major idea of Nixon's that he had heeded. Eisenhower responded with the flip comment, "If you give me a week, I might think of one."<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> Although both Eisenhower and Nixon later claimed that he was merely joking with the reporter, the remark hurt Nixon, as it undercut his claims of having greater decision-making experience than Kennedy. The remark proved so damaging to Nixon that the Democrats turned Eisenhower's statement into a television commercial.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>At the Republican National Convention, Nixon had pledged to campaign in all fifty states. This pledge backfired when, in August, Nixon injured his knee on a car door while campaigning in <a href="/wiki/North_Carolina" title="North Carolina">North Carolina</a>. The knee became infected and Nixon had to cease campaigning for two weeks while the infection was treated with <a href="/wiki/Antibacterial" class="mw-redirect" title="Antibacterial">antibiotics</a>. When he left <a href="/wiki/Walter_Reed_Army_Medical_Center" title="Walter Reed Army Medical Center">Walter Reed Hospital</a>, Nixon refused to abandon his pledge to visit every state; he thus wound up wasting valuable time visiting states that he had no chance of winning, or that had few electoral votes and would be of little help at the election, or states that he would almost certainly win regardless. For example, in his effort to visit all 50 states, Nixon spent the vital weekend before the election campaigning in Alaska, which had only three electoral votes, while Kennedy campaigned in more populous states, such as New Jersey, Ohio, Michigan, and Pennsylvania.
</p><p>Nixon visited Atlanta, Georgia on August 26 and acquired a very large turnout to his event. Nixon rode through a parade in Atlanta and was greeted by 150,000 people.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Nixon mentioned in his speech in Atlanta, "in the last quarter of a century there hasn't been a Democratic candidate for President that has bothered to campaign in the State of Georgia."<sup id="cite_ref-presidency.ucsb.edu_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-presidency.ucsb.edu-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> However, Kennedy would not let Nixon take the Democratic states that easily. Kennedy would change that statistic and visit some surprising states, including Georgia. Senator Kennedy visited the cities of Columbus, Warm Springs, and Lagrange on his campaign trail in Georgia. In his visit to Warm Springs, state troopers tried to keep Kennedy from an immense crowd, however Kennedy reached out to shake hands of those who were sick with polio.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> He also visited small towns across Georgia on his trip and saw a mass of 100,000 people during the entire visit. Kennedy spoke at a rehabilitation facility in Warm Springs. Warm Springs was near and dear to Kennedy's heart due to the effects the facility had on <a href="/wiki/Franklin_D._Roosevelt" title="Franklin D. Roosevelt">Franklin D. Roosevelt</a>. Roosevelt spent time at the rehabilitation facility and died there in 1945.<sup id="cite_ref-presidency.ucsb.edu_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-presidency.ucsb.edu-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In Warm Springs, Kennedy spoke to supporters at the facility and mentioned Franklin D. Roosevelt in his speech. He admired Roosevelt and commended him for sticking up for the farmers, workers, small towns, big cities, those in poverty, and those who were sick.<sup id="cite_ref-presidency.ucsb.edu_24-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-presidency.ucsb.edu-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> He said Roosevelt had a "spirit of strength and progress to get America moving."<sup id="cite_ref-presidency.ucsb.edu_24-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-presidency.ucsb.edu-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Kennedy discussed his six-point plan for healthcare. He wanted a medical program set up for retirement and federal funding for the construction of medical schools and hospitals. He also planned for the government to loan students money to attend medical school and provide grants to renovate old hospitals. He called for more money to be spent on medical research and finally, expand effort for rehabilitation and come up with new ways to assist those in need.<sup id="cite_ref-presidency.ucsb.edu_24-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-presidency.ucsb.edu-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Many Republicans disapproved Kennedy's plans and described them as an "appeal to socialism".<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> People around Warm Springs were supportive of Kennedy. Women were wearing hats reading, "Kennedy and Johnson".<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> Signs around the town read, "Douglas County For Kennedy Except 17 Republicans 6 Old Grouches".<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> Joe O. Butts, the mayor of Warm Springs during Kennedy's visit, said, "He must've shaken hands with everybody within two miles of him, and he was smiling all the time."<sup id="cite_ref-:1_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Despite the reservations Robert Kennedy had about Johnson's nomination, choosing Johnson as Kennedy's running mate proved to be a masterstroke. Johnson vigorously campaigned for Kennedy and was instrumental in helping the Democrats to carry several Southern states skeptical of him, especially Johnson's home state of Texas. Johnson made a "last-minute change of plans and scheduled two 12-minute whistlestop speeches in Georgia".<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> One of these visits included stopping in Atlanta to speak from the rear of a train at Terminal Station.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> On the other hand, Ambassador Lodge, Nixon's running mate, ran a lethargic campaign and made several mistakes that hurt Nixon. Among them was a pledge—not approved by Nixon—that as president, Nixon would name a Black person to his cabinet. The remark offended many Blacks who saw it as a clumsy attempt to win their votes.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Debates">Debates</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=1960_United_States_presidential_election&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Debates">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There were four presidential debates and no vice presidential debates during the 1960 general election.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>
