data-height=&quot;360&quot; data-bandwidth=&quot;310488&quot; data-framerate=&quot;29.97&quot;/&gt;&lt;source src=&quot;//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/c/c5/John_F._Kennedy_and_Richard_Nixon_-_United_States_presidential_TV_debate_%28September_26%2C_1960%29.webm/John_F._Kennedy_and_Richard_Nixon_-_United_States_presidential_TV_debate_%28September_26%2C_1960%29.webm.360p.webm&quot; type=&quot;video/webm; codecs=&amp;quot;vp8, vorbis&amp;quot;&quot; data-title=&quot;WebM (360P)&quot; data-shorttitle=&quot;WebM 360P&quot; data-transcodekey=&quot;360p.webm&quot; data-width=&quot;640&quot; data-height=&quot;360&quot; data-bandwidth=&quot;572976&quot; data-framerate=&quot;29.97&quot;/&gt;&lt;/video&gt;&lt;/div&gt;"><img alt="File:John F. Kennedy and Richard Nixon - United States presidential TV debate (September 26, 1960).webm" style="width:220px;height:124px" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/John_F._Kennedy_and_Richard_Nixon_-_United_States_presidential_TV_debate_%28September_26%2C_1960%29.webm/220px--John_F._Kennedy_and_Richard_Nixon_-_United_States_presidential_TV_debate_%28September_26%2C_1960%29.webm.jpg" /><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c5/John_F._Kennedy_and_Richard_Nixon_-_United_States_presidential_TV_debate_%28September_26%2C_1960%29.webm" title="Play media" target="new"><span class="play-btn-large"><span class="mw-tmh-playtext">Play media</span></span></a></div>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:John_F._Kennedy_and_Richard_Nixon_-_United_States_presidential_TV_debate_(September_26,_1960).webm" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Excerpts from the TV debate of September 26, 1960</div></div></div><p>The key turning point of the campaign came with the four Kennedy-Nixon debates; they were the first presidential debates ever (The <a href="/wiki/Lincoln%E2%80%93Douglas_debates" title="Lincoln–Douglas debates">Lincoln–Douglas debates</a> of 1858 had been the first for senators from Illinois), also the first held on television, and thus attracted enormous publicity. Nixon insisted on campaigning until just a few hours before the first debate started. He had not completely recovered from his stay in hospital and thus looked pale, sickly, underweight, and tired.<sup id="cite_ref-Nixon,_p._270_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nixon,_p._270-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> His eyes moved across the room during the debate, and at various moments sweat was visible on his face. He also refused make-up for the first debate, and as a result his facial stubble showed prominently on the black-and-white TV screens at the time. Furthermore, the debate set appeared darker once the paint dried up, causing Nixon's suit color to blend in with the background which reduced his stature.<sup id="cite_ref-Nixon,_p._270_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nixon,_p._270-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> Nixon's poor appearance on television in the first debate is reflected by the fact that his mother called him immediately following the debate to ask if he was sick.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-addisons_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-addisons-36">&#91;note 1&#93;</a></sup> Kennedy, by contrast, rested and prepared extensively beforehand, appearing tanned,<sup id="cite_ref-addisons_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-addisons-36">&#91;note 1&#93;</a></sup> confident, and relaxed during the debate.<sup id="cite_ref-schlinder_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-schlinder-37">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> An estimated 70 million viewers watched the first debate.<sup id="cite_ref-museum.tv_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-museum.tv-38">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>It is often claimed that people who watched the debate on television overwhelmingly believed Kennedy had won, while radio listeners (a smaller audience) thought Nixon had ended up defeating him.<sup id="cite_ref-museum.tv_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-museum.tv-38">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> However, that has been disputed.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> Indeed, one study has speculated that the viewer/listener disagreement could be due to sample bias in that those without TV could be a skewed subset of the population:<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886047036">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Evidence in support of this belief [<i>i.e.</i>, that Kennedy's physical appearance overshadowed his performance during the first debate] is mainly limited to sketchy reports about a market survey conducted by Sindlinger &amp; Company in which 49% of those who listened to the debates on radio said Nixon had won compared to 21% naming Kennedy, while 30% of those who watched the debates on television said Kennedy had won compared to 29% naming Nixon. Contrary to popular belief, the Sindlinger evidence suggests not that Kennedy won on television but that the candidates tied on television while Nixon won on radio. However, no details about the sample have ever been reported, and it is unclear whether the survey results can be generalized to a larger population. Moreover, since 87% of American households had a television in 1960 [and that the] fraction of Americans lacking access to television in 1960 was concentrated in rural areas and particularly in southern and western states, places that were unlikely to hold significant proportions of Catholic voters.<sup id="cite_ref-schlinder_37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-schlinder-37">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<p>After the first debate, polls showed Kennedy moving from a slight deficit into a slight lead over Nixon. For the remaining three debates, Nixon regained his lost weight, wore television makeup, and appeared more forceful than in his initial appearance.
</p><p>However, up to 20 million fewer viewers watched the three remaining debates than the first. Political observers at the time felt that Kennedy won the first debate,<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> Nixon won the second<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> and third debates,<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> while the fourth debate,<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> which was seen as the strongest performance by both men, was a draw.
</p><p>The third debate has been notable, as it brought about a change in the debate process. This debate was a monumental step for television. For the first time ever, split-screen technology was used to bring two people from opposite sides of the country together so they were able to converse in real time. Nixon was in Los Angeles while Kennedy was in New York. The men appear to be in the same room, thanks to identical sets. Both candidates had monitors in their respective studios containing the feed from the opposite studio so they could respond to questions. Bill Shadel moderated the debate from a third television studio in Chicago. The main topic of this debate was whether military force should be used to prevent <a href="/wiki/Kinmen" title="Kinmen">Quemoy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Matsu_Islands" title="Matsu Islands">Matsu</a>, two island archipelagos off the Chinese coast, from falling under Communist control.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Campaign_issues">Campaign issues</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=1960_United_States_presidential_election&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Campaign issues">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A key concern in Kennedy's campaign was the widespread skepticism among Protestants about his <a href="/wiki/20th_century_history_of_the_Catholic_Church_in_the_United_States" class="mw-redirect" title="20th century history of the Catholic Church in the United States">Roman Catholic</a> religion. Some Protestants, especially Southern Baptists and Lutherans, feared that having a Catholic in the White House would give undue influence to the Pope in the nation's affairs.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> Radio evangelists such as <a href="/wiki/G._E._Lowman" title="G. E. Lowman">G. E. Lowman</a> wrote that, "Each person has the right to their own religious belief  ... [but]  ... the Roman Catholic ecclesiastical system demands the first allegiance of every true member and says in a conflict between church and state, the <i>church</i> must prevail".<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> The religious issue was so significant that Kennedy made a speech before the nation's newspaper editors in which he criticized the prominence they gave to the religious issue over other topics – especially in foreign policy – that he felt were of greater importance.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>To address fears among Protestants that his Roman Catholicism would impact his decision-making, Kennedy told the Greater Houston Ministerial Association on September 12, 1960, "I am not the Catholic candidate for president. I am the Democratic Party's candidate for president who also happens to be a Catholic. I do not speak for my Church on public matters – and the Church does not speak for me."<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> He promised to respect the separation of church and state and not to allow Catholic officials to dictate public policy to him. Kennedy also raised the question of whether one-quarter of Americans were relegated to second-class citizenship just because they were Roman Catholic. Kennedy would become the first, and, as of 2018<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=1960_United_States_presidential_election&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, only Roman Catholic to be elected president.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Kennedy's campaign took advantage of an opening when Rev. <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther_King_Jr." title="Martin Luther King Jr.">Martin Luther King Jr.</a>, the civil-rights leader, was arrested in Georgia while taking part in a <a href="/wiki/Sit-in#Civil_Rights_Movement" title="Sit-in">sit-in</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> Nixon asked President <a href="/wiki/Dwight_D._Eisenhower" title="Dwight D. Eisenhower">Dwight D. Eisenhower</a> to pardon King, but the President declined to do so. Nixon refused to take further action, but Kennedy placed calls to local political authorities to get King released from jail, and he also called King's father and wife. As a result, King's father endorsed Kennedy, and he received much favorable publicity in the black community.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> A letter to the Governor of Georgia regarding Martin Luther King Jr.'s arrest also helped Kennedy garner many African American votes. John F. Kennedy asked Governor Ernest Vandiver to look into the harsh sentencing and stated his claim that he did not want to have to get involved in Georgia's justice system.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> A member of Kennedy's civil rights team and King's friend, Harris Wofford, and other Kennedy campaign members passed out a pamphlet to black churchgoers the Sunday before the presidential election that said, ""<i>No Comment" Nixon versus a Candidate with a Heart, Senator Kennedy."</i>"<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> On election day, Kennedy won the black vote in most areas by wide margins, and this may have provided his margin of victory in states such as New Jersey, South Carolina, Illinois, and Missouri.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Researchers found that Kennedy's appeal to African American voters appears to be largely responsible for his receiving more African-American votes than Adlai Stevenson in the 1956 election. The same study conducted found that white voters were less influenced on the topic of civil rights than black voters in 1960. The Republican national chairman at the time, <a href="/wiki/Thruston_Ballard_Morton" title="Thruston Ballard Morton">Thruston Ballard Morton</a>, regarded the African-American vote as the single most crucial factor.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The issue that dominated the election was the rising Cold War tensions between the United States and the Soviet Union.<sup id="cite_ref-jfk1960_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jfk1960-56">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> In 1957, the Soviets had launched Sputnik, the first man-made satellite to orbit Earth.<sup id="cite_ref-jfk1960_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jfk1960-56">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> Soon afterwards, some American leaders warned that the nation was falling behind communist countries in science and technology.<sup id="cite_ref-jfk1960_56-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jfk1960-56">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> In Cuba, the revolutionary regime of Fidel Castro, became a close ally of the Soviet Union in 1960, heightening fears of communist subversion in the Western Hemisphere.<sup id="cite_ref-jfk1960_56-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jfk1960-56">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>  Public opinion polls revealed that more than half the American people thought war with the Soviet Union was inevitable.<sup id="cite_ref-jfk1960_56-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jfk1960-56">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Kennedy took advantage of increased Cold War tension by emphasizing a perceived "<a href="/wiki/Missile_gap" title="Missile gap">missile gap</a>" between the United States and Soviet Union. He argued that under the Republicans the Soviets had developed a major advantage in the numbers of nuclear missiles.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>  He proposed a bipartisan congressional investigation about the possibility that the Soviet Union was ahead of the United States in developing missiles.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> He also noted in an October 18 speech that several senior US military officers had long criticized the Eisenhower Administration's defense spending policies.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Both candidates also argued about the economy and ways in which they could increase the economic growth and prosperity of the 1950s and make it accessible to more people (especially minorities). Some historians criticize Nixon for not taking greater advantage of Eisenhower's popularity (which was around 60–65% throughout 1960 and on election day) and for not discussing the prosperous economy of the Eisenhower presidency more often in his campaign.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> As the campaign moved into the final two weeks, the polls and most political pundits predicted a Kennedy victory. However, President Eisenhower, who had largely sat out the campaign, made a vigorous campaign tour for Nixon over the last 10 days before the election. Eisenhower's support gave Nixon a badly needed boost. Nixon also criticized Kennedy for stating that <a href="/wiki/Quemoy" class="mw-redirect" title="Quemoy">Quemoy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Matsu_Islands" title="Matsu Islands">Matsu</a>, two small islands off the coast of Communist China that were held by Nationalist Chinese forces based in Taiwan, were outside the treaty of protection the United States had signed with the Nationalist Chinese. Nixon claimed the islands were included in the treaty and accused Kennedy of showing weakness towards Communist aggression.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> Aided by the Quemoy and Matsu issue, and by Eisenhower's support, Nixon began to gain momentum and by election day the polls indicated a virtual tie.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Results">Results</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=1960_United_States_presidential_election&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Results">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a href="/wiki/File:PresidentialCounty1960Colorbrewer.gif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/PresidentialCounty1960Colorbrewer.gif/400px-PresidentialCounty1960Colorbrewer.gif" decoding="async" width="400" height="243" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/PresidentialCounty1960Colorbrewer.gif/600px-PresidentialCounty1960Colorbrewer.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/PresidentialCounty1960Colorbrewer.gif/800px-PresidentialCounty1960Colorbrewer.gif 2x" data-file-width="2800" data-file-height="1700" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:PresidentialCounty1960Colorbrewer.gif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Results by county explicitly indicating the percentage for the winning candidate. Shades of blue are for Kennedy (Democratic), shades of red are for Nixon (Republican), and shades of green are for <a href="/wiki/Unpledged_elector" title="Unpledged elector">Unpledged Electors</a> (Democratic/States' Rights).</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1960_Presidential_Election_in_the_United_States,_Results_by_Congressional_District.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/1960_Presidential_Election_in_the_United_States%2C_Results_by_Congressional_District.png/400px-1960_Presidential_Election_in_the_United_States%2C_Results_by_Congressional_District.png" decoding="async" width="400" height="258" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/1960_Presidential_Election_in_the_United_States%2C_Results_by_Congressional_District.png/600px-1960_Presidential_Election_in_the_United_States%2C_Results_by_Congressional_District.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/1960_Presidential_Election_in_the_United_States%2C_Results_by_Congressional_District.png/800px-1960_Presidential_Election_in_the_United_States%2C_Results_by_Congressional_District.png 2x" data-file-width="1547" data-file-height="997" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1960_Presidential_Election_in_the_United_States,_Results_by_Congressional_District.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Results by congressional district.</div></div></div>
<p>The election was held on November 8, 1960. Nixon watched the election returns from his suite at the <a href="/wiki/Ambassador_Hotel_(Los_Angeles)" title="Ambassador Hotel (Los Angeles)">Ambassador Hotel</a> in Los Angeles, while Kennedy watched them at the <a href="/wiki/Kennedy_Compound" title="Kennedy Compound">Kennedy Compound</a> in <a href="/wiki/Hyannis_Port,_Massachusetts" title="Hyannis Port, Massachusetts">Hyannis Port, Massachusetts</a>. As the early returns poured in from large Northeastern and Midwestern cities, such as <a href="/wiki/Boston" title="Boston">Boston</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a>, <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia" title="Philadelphia">Philadelphia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pittsburgh" title="Pittsburgh">Pittsburgh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cleveland" title="Cleveland">Cleveland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Detroit" title="Detroit">Detroit</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Chicago" title="Chicago">Chicago</a>, Kennedy opened a large lead in the popular and electoral votes, and appeared headed for victory. However, as later returns came in from <a href="/wiki/Rural" class="mw-redirect" title="Rural">rural</a> and <a href="/wiki/Suburban" class="mw-redirect" title="Suburban">suburban</a> areas in the <a href="/wiki/Midwestern_United_States" title="Midwestern United States">Midwest</a>, the Rocky Mountain states and the Pacific Coast states, Nixon began to steadily close the gap on Kennedy.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Before midnight, <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i> had gone to press with the headline "Kennedy Elected President". As the election again became too close to call, <i>Times</i> managing editor <a href="/wiki/Turner_Catledge" title="Turner Catledge">Turner Catledge</a> hoped that, as he recalled in his memoirs, "a certain Midwestern mayor would steal enough votes to pull Kennedy through", thus allowing the <i>Times</i> to avoid the embarrassment of announcing the wrong winner, as the <i><a href="/wiki/Chicago_Tribune" title="Chicago Tribune">Chicago Tribune</a></i> had memorably <a href="/wiki/Dewey_Defeats_Truman" title="Dewey Defeats Truman">done</a> <a href="/wiki/1948_United_States_presidential_election" title="1948 United States presidential election">twelve years earlier</a> in announcing that <a href="/wiki/Thomas_E._Dewey" title="Thomas E. Dewey">Thomas E. Dewey</a> had defeated <a href="/wiki/Harry_S._Truman" title="Harry S. Truman">President Truman</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-wash_post_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wash_post-62">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Nixon made a speech at about 3&#160;a.m., and hinted that Kennedy might have won the election. News reporters were puzzled, as it was not a formal concession speech. It was not until the afternoon of the next day that Nixon finally conceded the election, and Kennedy claimed his victory.
</p><p>Of the 3,129 counties and independent cities making returns, Nixon won in 1,857 (59.35%) while Kennedy carried 1,200 (38.35%). "Unpledged" electors came first in 71 counties (2.27%) throughout Louisiana and Mississippi and one county (0.03%) in Alaska split evenly between Kennedy and Nixon.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:John_F._Kennedy_voting_1960.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/John_F._Kennedy_voting_1960.jpg/220px-John_F._Kennedy_voting_1960.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="265" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/John_F._Kennedy_voting_1960.jpg/330px-John_F._Kennedy_voting_1960.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/John_F._Kennedy_voting_1960.jpg/440px-John_F._Kennedy_voting_1960.jpg 2x" data-file-width="998" data-file-height="1200" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:John_F._Kennedy_voting_1960.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Kennedy cast his ballot at his <a href="/wiki/Polling_place" title="Polling place">polling place</a> at a branch of the <a href="/wiki/Boston_Public_Library" title="Boston Public Library">Boston Public Library</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>A sample of how close the election was can be seen in California, Nixon's home state. Kennedy seemed to have carried the state by 37,000 votes when all of the voting precincts reported, but when the <a href="/wiki/Absentee_ballots" class="mw-redirect" title="Absentee ballots">absentee ballots</a> were counted a week later, Nixon came from behind to win the state by 36,000 votes.<sup id="cite_ref-salon_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-salon-63">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> Similarly, in Hawaii, it appeared as though Nixon had won there (it was actually called for him early Wednesday morning), but in a recount, Kennedy was able to come from behind and win the state by an extremely narrow margin of 115 votes.
</p><p>In the national popular vote, Kennedy beat Nixon by less than two tenths of one percentage point (0.17%)—the closest popular-vote margin of the 20th century. In the <a href="/wiki/Electoral_College_(United_States)" class="mw-redirect" title="Electoral College (United States)">Electoral College</a>, Kennedy's victory was larger, as he took 303 electoral votes to Nixon's 219; 269 were needed to win. A total of 15 electors—eight from Mississippi, six from Alabama, and one from Oklahoma—refused to vote for either Kennedy or Nixon. Instead, they cast their votes for Senator <a href="/wiki/Harry_F._Byrd" title="Harry F. Byrd">Harry F. Byrd</a> of Virginia, a conservative Democrat, even though he had not been a candidate for president.<sup id="cite_ref-Dudley_Shiraev_2008_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dudley_Shiraev_2008-64">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> Kennedy carried 12 states by three percentage points or less, while Nixon won six by similarly narrow margins. Kennedy carried all but three states in the populous <a href="/wiki/Northeastern_United_States" title="Northeastern United States">Northeast</a>, and he also carried the large states of Michigan, Illinois, and Missouri in the Midwest. With Lyndon Johnson's help, he also carried most of the South, including the large states of North Carolina, Georgia, and Texas. Nixon carried all but three of the Western states (including California), and he ran strong in the farm belt states, where his biggest victory was in Ohio.
</p><p><i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i>, summarizing the discussion in late November, spoke of a "narrow consensus" among the experts that Kennedy had won more than he lost "as a result of his Catholicism",<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> as Northern Catholics flocked to Kennedy because of attacks on his religion. Interviewing people who voted in both 1956 and 1960, a <a href="/wiki/University_of_Michigan" title="University of Michigan">University of Michigan</a> team analyzing the election returns discovered that people who voted Democratic in 1956 split 33–6 for Kennedy, while the Republican voters of 1956 split 44–17 for Nixon. That is, Nixon lost 28% (<span role="math" class="sfrac nowrap tion" style="display:inline-block; vertical-align:-0.5em; font-size:85%; text-align:center;"><span class="num" style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em;">17</span><span class="slash visualhide">/</span><span class="den" style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em; border-top:1px solid;">61</span></span>) of the Eisenhower voters, while Kennedy lost only 15% of the Stevenson voters. The Democrats, in other words, did a better job of holding their 1956 supporters.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Kennedy said that he saw the challenges ahead and needed the country's support to get through them. In his victory speech, he declared, "To all Americans, I say that the next four years are going to be difficult and challenging years for us all; that a supreme national effort will be needed to move this country safely through the 1960s. I ask your help and I can assure you that every degree of my spirit that I possess will be devoted to the long-range interest of the United States and to the cause of freedom around the world."<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Controversies">Controversies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=1960_United_States_presidential_election&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Controversies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Some, including Republican legislators and journalists, believed that Kennedy benefited from <a href="/wiki/Vote_fraud" class="mw-redirect" title="Vote fraud">vote fraud</a>, especially in Texas, where his running mate Lyndon B. Johnson was senator, and Illinois, home of Mayor <a href="/wiki/Richard_J._Daley" title="Richard J. Daley">Richard Daley</a>'s powerful <a href="/wiki/Chicago_political_machine" class="mw-redirect" title="Chicago political machine">Chicago political machine</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-salon_63-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-salon-63">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> These two states were important because if Nixon had carried both, he would have earned 270 electoral votes, one more than the 269 needed to win the majority in the Electoral College and the presidency. Republican senators such as <a href="/wiki/Everett_Dirksen" title="Everett Dirksen">Everett Dirksen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Barry_Goldwater" title="Barry Goldwater">Barry Goldwater</a> also thought that vote fraud "played a role in the election",<sup id="cite_ref-wash_post_62-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wash_post-62">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> and that Nixon actually won the national popular vote. Republicans tried and failed to overturn the results in both Illinois and Texas at the time—as well as in nine other states.<sup id="cite_ref-greenberg_slate_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-greenberg_slate-68">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> Some journalists also later claimed that mobster <a href="/wiki/Sam_Giancana" title="Sam Giancana">Sam Giancana</a> and his <a href="/wiki/Chicago_Outfit" title="Chicago Outfit">Chicago crime syndicate</a> "played a role" in Kennedy's victory in Illinois.<sup id="cite_ref-greenberg_slate_68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-greenberg_slate-68">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Nixon's campaign staff urged him to pursue recounts and challenge the validity of Kennedy's victory in several states, especially in Illinois, Missouri, and New Jersey, where large majorities in Catholic precincts handed Kennedy the election.<sup id="cite_ref-wash_post_62-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wash_post-62">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> However, Nixon gave a speech three days after the election stating that he would not contest the election.<sup id="cite_ref-wash_post_62-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wash_post-62">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> The Republican National Chairman, Senator <a href="/wiki/Thruston_Ballard_Morton" title="Thruston Ballard Morton">Thruston Ballard Morton</a> of Kentucky, visited <a href="/wiki/Key_Biscayne,_Florida" title="Key Biscayne, Florida">Key Biscayne, Florida</a>, where Nixon had taken his family for a vacation, and pushed for a recount.<sup id="cite_ref-wash_post_62-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wash_post-62">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> Morton did challenge the results in 11 states,<sup id="cite_ref-salon_63-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-salon-63">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> keeping challenges in the courts into the summer of 1961; however, the only result of these challenges was the loss of Hawaii to Kennedy on a recount.
</p><p>Kennedy won Illinois by less than 9,000 votes out of 4.75 million cast, or a margin of 0.2%.<sup id="cite_ref-salon_63-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-salon-63">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> However, Nixon carried 92 of the state's 101 counties. Kennedy's victory in Illinois came from the city of Chicago, which boasted favorable demographics for the Democrat, with its large populations of <a href="/wiki/Catholic" class="mw-redirect" title="Catholic">Catholic</a> and <a href="/wiki/African-American" class="mw-redirect" title="African-American">African-American</a> voters.<sup id="cite_ref-wash_post_von_hippel_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wash_post_von_hippel-69">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> His victory margin in the city was 318,736 and 456,312 in Cook County. A myth arose that Chicago Mayor Richard J. Daley held back much of the city's vote until the late morning hours of November 9. Yet when the Republican <a href="/wiki/Chicago_Tribune" title="Chicago Tribune">Chicago Tribune</a> went to press, 79 percent of Cook County precincts had reported, compared with just 62 percent of Illinois's precincts overall. Moreover, Nixon never led in Illinois, and Kennedy's lead merely shrank as election night went on.<sup id="cite_ref-wash_post_von_hippel_69-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wash_post_von_hippel-69">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Earl_Mazo" title="Earl Mazo">Earl Mazo</a>, a reporter for the pro-Nixon <i><a href="/wiki/New_York_Herald_Tribune" title="New York Herald Tribune">New York Herald Tribune</a></i> and his biographer, investigated the voting in Chicago and "claimed to have discovered sufficient evidence of vote fraud to prove that the state was stolen for Kennedy."<sup id="cite_ref-salon_63-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-salon-63">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In Texas, Kennedy defeated Nixon by a 51 to 49% margin, or 46,000 votes.<sup id="cite_ref-salon_63-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-salon-63">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> Some Republicans argued that Johnson's formidable political machine had stolen enough votes in counties along the <a href="/wiki/Mexico%E2%80%93United_States_border" title="Mexico–United States border">Mexican border</a> to give Kennedy the victory. Kennedy's defenders, such as his speechwriter and special assistant <a href="/wiki/Arthur_M._Schlesinger,_Jr." class="mw-redirect" title="Arthur M. Schlesinger, Jr.">Arthur M. Schlesinger, Jr.</a>, have argued that Kennedy's margin in Texas was simply too large for vote fraud to have been a decisive factor. Russell D. Renka, a former political science professor at Southeastern Missouri State University,<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> acknowledged that it was more than likely that Johnson's political machine in the state's lower Rio Grande Valley counties, including the notorious <a href="/wiki/Duval_County,_Texas" title="Duval County, Texas">Duval County</a>, could have "managed to produce a significant number of forged votes" for Kennedy.<sup id="cite_ref-renka1960_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-renka1960-71">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> However, Renka also acknowledged that Kennedy's margin in the state's initial tally "made it far too difficult to prove that voter fraud had determined who won Texas" and that "any recount would also have been hard to conduct."<sup id="cite_ref-renka1960_71-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-renka1960-71">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Allegations of voter fraud were made in Texas. For example, <a href="/wiki/Fannin_County,_Texas" title="Fannin County, Texas">Fannin County</a> had only 4,895 registered voters, yet 6,138 votes were cast in that county, three-quarters for Kennedy.<sup id="cite_ref-wash_post_62-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wash_post-62">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> In an <a href="/wiki/Angelina_County,_Texas" title="Angelina County, Texas">Angelina County</a> precinct, Kennedy received 187 votes to Nixon's 24, though there were only a total of 86 registered voters in the precinct.<sup id="cite_ref-wash_post_62-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wash_post-62">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>  When Republicans demanded a statewide recount, they learned that the state Board of Elections, whose members were all Democrats, had already "certified" Kennedy as the official winner in Texas.<sup id="cite_ref-wash_post_62-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wash_post-62">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> This analysis, though, ignores the fact that the registered voter figures measured only individuals who paid the <a href="/wiki/Poll_taxes_in_the_United_States" title="Poll taxes in the United States">poll tax</a> and that certain groups were exempt from having to pay that tax.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In Illinois, Schlesinger and others have pointed out that, even if Nixon had carried Illinois, the state alone would not have given him the victory, as Kennedy would still have won 276 electoral votes to Nixon's 246 (with 269 needed to win). More to the point, Illinois was the site of the most extensive challenge process, which fell short despite repeated efforts spearheaded by Cook County state's attorney, Benjamin Adamowski, a Republican, who also lost his re-election bid. Despite demonstrating net errors favoring both Nixon and Adamowski (some precincts—40% in Nixon's case—showed errors favoring them, a factor suggesting error, rather than fraud), the totals found fell short of reversing the results for either candidate. While a Daley-connected circuit judge, Thomas Kluczynski (who would later be appointed a federal judge by Kennedy, at Daley's recommendation), threw out a federal lawsuit "filed to contend" the voting totals,<sup id="cite_ref-wash_post_62-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wash_post-62">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> the Republican-dominated State Board of Elections unanimously rejected the challenge to the results. Furthermore, there were signs of possible irregularities in downstate areas controlled by Republicans, which Democrats never seriously pressed, since the Republican challenges went nowhere.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> More than a month after the election, the Republican National Committee abandoned its Illinois voter fraud claims.<sup id="cite_ref-salon_63-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-salon-63">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>However, a special prosecutor assigned to the case brought charges against 650 people, which did not result in convictions.<sup id="cite_ref-wash_post_62-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wash_post-62">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> Three Chicago election workers were convicted of voter fraud in 1962 and served short terms in jail.<sup id="cite_ref-wash_post_62-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wash_post-62">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> Mazo, the <i>Herald-Tribune</i> reporter, later said that he "found names of the dead who had voted in Chicago, along with 56 people from one house."<sup id="cite_ref-wash_post_62-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wash_post-62">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> He found cases of Republican voter fraud in southern Illinois, but said that the totals "did not match the Chicago fraud he found."<sup id="cite_ref-wash_post_62-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wash_post-62">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> After Mazo had published four parts of an intended 12-part voter fraud series documenting his findings, which was re-published nationally, he said, "Nixon requested his publisher stop the rest of the series so as to prevent a <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_crisis" title="Constitutional crisis">constitutional crisis</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-wash_post_62-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wash_post-62">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> Nevertheless, the <i><a href="/wiki/Chicago_Tribune" title="Chicago Tribune">Chicago Tribune</a></i> (which routinely endorsed GOP presidential candidates, including Nixon in 1960, 1968 and 1972) wrote that "the election of November 8 was characterized by such gross and palpable fraud as to justify the conclusion that [Nixon] was deprived of victory."<sup id="cite_ref-wash_post_62-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wash_post-62">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> Had Nixon won both Illinois and Texas, he would have ended up with exactly 270 electoral votes and the presidency, with or without a victory in the popular vote.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Popular_votes">Popular votes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=1960_United_States_presidential_election&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Popular votes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Alabama">Alabama</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=1960_United_States_presidential_election&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Alabama">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election_in_Alabama,_1960" class="mw-redirect" title="United States presidential election in Alabama, 1960">United States presidential election in Alabama, 1960</a> and <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_elections_in_which_the_winner_lost_the_popular_vote#1960_Alabama_results_ambiguity" title="United States presidential elections in which the winner lost the popular vote">United States presidential elections in which the winner lost the popular vote §&#160;1960 Alabama results ambiguity</a></div>
<p>The actual number of popular votes received by Kennedy in Alabama is difficult to determine because of the unusual situation in that state. Instead of having the voters choose from slates of electors, the Alabama ballot had voters choose the <a href="/wiki/US_Presidential_Electors" class="mw-redirect" title="US Presidential Electors">electors</a> individually. Traditionally, in such a situation, a given candidate is assigned the popular vote of the elector who received the most votes. For instance, candidates pledged to Nixon received anywhere from 230,951 votes (for George Witcher) to 237,981 votes (for Cecil Durham); Nixon is therefore assigned 237,981 popular votes from Alabama.
</p><p>The situation was more complicated on the Democratic side. The statewide Democratic primary had chosen eleven candidates for the Electoral College, five of whom were pledged to vote for Kennedy, and six of whom were free to vote for anyone they chose. All of these candidates won in the general election, and all six unpledged electors voted against Kennedy. The actual number of popular votes received by Kennedy is therefore difficult to allocate. Traditionally, Kennedy is assigned either 318,303 votes (the votes won by the most popular Kennedy elector) or 324,050 votes (the votes won by the most popular unpledged Democratic elector); the results table below is based on Kennedy winning 318,303 votes in Alabama.<sup id="cite_ref-trende_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-trende-74">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Georgia">Georgia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=1960_United_States_presidential_election&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Georgia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>The actual number of popular votes received by Kennedy and Nixon in Georgia is also difficult to determine because voters voted for 12 separate electors.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> The vote totals of 458,638 votes for Kennedy and 274,472 votes for Nixon reflect the number of votes for the Kennedy and Nixon electors who received the highest number of votes. However, the Republican and Democratic electors receiving the highest number of votes were <a href="/wiki/Outliers" class="mw-redirect" title="Outliers">outliers</a> from the other 11 electors from their party. The average vote totals for the 12 electors were 455,629 votes for the Democratic electors and 273,110 votes for the Republican electors. This shrinks Kennedy's election margin in Georgia by 1,647 votes to 182,519.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Unpledged_Democratic_electors">Unpledged Democratic electors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=1960_United_States_presidential_election&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Unpledged Democratic electors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Unpledged_elector" title="Unpledged elector">Unpledged elector</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Harry_F._Byrd.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Harry_F._Byrd.jpg/200px-Harry_F._Byrd.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="250" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Harry_F._Byrd.jpg/300px-Harry_F._Byrd.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Harry_F._Byrd.jpg/400px-Harry_F._Byrd.jpg 2x" data-file-width="760" data-file-height="950" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Harry_F._Byrd.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Senator Harry F. Byrd, Sr. received 15 electoral votes</div></div></div>
<p>Many Southern Democrats were opposed to voting rights for <a href="/wiki/African_American" class="mw-redirect" title="African American">African Americans</a> living in the South. There was a call from segregationists for electoral votes to be withheld or to be cast to Harry F. Byrd, an independent candidate.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> Both before and after the convention, they attempted to put <a href="/wiki/Unpledged_elector" title="Unpledged elector">unpledged Democratic electors</a> on their states' ballots in the hopes of influencing the race: the existence of such electors might influence which candidate would be chosen by the national convention, and, in a close race, such electors might be in a position to extract concessions from either the Democratic or Republican presidential candidates in return for their electoral votes.
</p><p>Most of these attempts failed. Alabama put up a mixed slate of five loyal electors and six unpledged electors. Mississippi put up two distinct slates, one of loyalists and one of unpledged electors. Louisiana also put up two distinct slates, although the unpledged slate did not receive the "Democratic" label. Georgia freed its Democratic electors from pledges to vote for Kennedy. Governor <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Vandiver" title="Ernest Vandiver">Ernest Vandiver</a> supported the unpledged electoral votes. Former Governor Ellis Arnall did not support the unpledged electoral votes. Arnall called Vandiver's stand, "utterly disgraceful."<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In total, fourteen <a href="/wiki/Unpledged_elector" title="Unpledged elector">unpledged Democratic electors</a> won election from the voters. Because electors pledged to Kennedy had won a clear majority of the Electoral College, the unpledged electors could not influence the results. Nonetheless, they refused to vote for Kennedy. Instead they voted for <a href="/wiki/Virginia" title="Virginia">Virginia</a> Senator <a href="/wiki/Harry_F._Byrd" title="Harry F. Byrd">Harry F. Byrd</a>, a segregationist Democrat, even though Byrd was not an announced candidate and did not seek their votes. In addition, Byrd received one electoral vote from a faithless Oklahoma elector, for a total of 15 electoral votes. The faithless Oklahoma elector voted for <a href="/wiki/Barry_Goldwater" title="Barry Goldwater">Barry Goldwater</a> as vice president; the other 14 voted for <a href="/wiki/Strom_Thurmond" title="Strom Thurmond">Strom Thurmond</a> as vice president.
</p>

<p>There were 537 electoral votes, up from 531 in 1956, because of the addition of two U.S. Senators and one U.S. Representative from each of the new states of Alaska and Hawaii. (The House of Representatives was temporarily expanded from 435 members to 437 to accommodate this, and went back to 435 when reapportioned according to the 1960 census.  The reapportionment did not take place until after the 1960 election.)

<sup>(b)</sup> <i>Byrd was not directly on the ballot. Instead, his electoral votes came from <a href="/wiki/Unpledged_Elector" class="mw-redirect" title="Unpledged Elector">unpledged Democratic electors</a> and a faithless elector.</i><br />
<sup>(c)</sup> <i>Oklahoma <a href="/wiki/Faithless_elector" title="Faithless elector">faithless elector</a> <a href="/wiki/Henry_D._Irwin" title="Henry D. Irwin">Henry D. Irwin</a>, though pledged to vote for Richard Nixon and Henry Cabot Lodge Jr., instead voted for non-candidate <a href="/wiki/Harry_F._Byrd" title="Harry F. Byrd">Harry F. Byrd</a>. However, unlike other electors who voted for Byrd and <a href="/wiki/Strom_Thurmond" title="Strom Thurmond">Strom Thurmond</a> as vice president, Irwin cast his vice presidential electoral vote for Arizona Republican Senator Barry Goldwater.<br /></i>
<sup>(d)</sup> <i>In Mississippi, the slate of unpledged Democratic electors won. They cast their 8 votes for Byrd and Thurmond.</i>
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Close_states">Close states</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=1960_United_States_presidential_election&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Close states">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Margin of victory less than 1% (95 electoral votes):
</p>
<ol><li><span style="color:blue;"><b>Hawaii</b>, 0.06% </span></li>
<li><span style="color:blue;"><b>Illinois</b>, 0.19% </span></li>
<li><span style="color:blue;"><b>Missouri</b>, 0.52% </span> (tipping point state for Kennedy win)</li>
<li><span style="color:red;"><b>California</b>, 0.55% </span></li>
<li><span style="color:blue;"><b>New Mexico</b>, 0.74% </span></li>
<li><span style="color:blue;"><b>New Jersey</b>, 0.80% </span> (tipping point state for Nixon win)</li></ol>
<p>Margin of victory less than 5% (161 electoral votes):
</p>
<ol><li><span style="color:blue;"><b>Minnesota</b>, 1.43% </span></li>
<li><span style="color:blue;"><b>Delaware</b>, 1.64% </span></li>
<li><span style="color:red;"><b>Alaska</b>, 1.88% </span></li>
<li><span style="color:blue;"><b>Texas</b>, 2.00% </span></li>
<li><span style="color:blue;"><b>Michigan</b>, 2.01% </span></li>
<li><span style="color:blue;"><b>Nevada</b>, 2.32% </span></li>
<li><span style="color:blue;"><b>Pennsylvania</b>, 2.32% </span></li>
<li><span style="color:red;"><b>Washington</b>, 2.41% </span></li>
<li><span style="color:blue;"><b>South Carolina</b>, 2.48% </span></li>
<li><span style="color:red;"><b>Montana</b>, 2.50% </span></li>
<li><span style="color:orange;"><b>Mississippi</b>, 2.64% </span></li>
<li><span style="color:red;"><b>Florida</b>, 3.03% </span></li>
<li><span style="color:red;"><b>Wisconsin</b>, 3.72% </span></li>
<li><span style="color:blue;"><b>North Carolina</b>, 4.22% </span></li></ol>
<p>Margin of victory over 5%, but under 10% (160 electoral votes):
</p>
<ol><li><span style="color:red;"><b>Oregon</b>, 5.24% </span></li>
<li><span style="color:blue;"><b>New York</b>, 5.26% </span></li>
<li><span style="color:blue;"><b>West Virginia</b>, 5.46% </span></li>
<li><span style="color:red;"><b>Virginia</b>, 5.47% </span></li>
<li><span style="color:red;"><b>Ohio</b>, 6.56% </span></li>
<li><span style="color:red;"><b>New Hampshire</b>, 6.84% </span></li>
<li><span style="color:blue;"><b>Arkansas</b>, 7.13% </span></li>
<li><span style="color:red;"><b>Tennessee</b>, 7.15% </span></li>
<li><span style="color:red;"><b>Kentucky</b>, 7.18% </span></li>
<li><span style="color:blue;"><b>Maryland</b>, 7.22% </span></li>
<li><span style="color:blue;"><b>Connecticut</b>, 7.46% </span></li>
<li><span style="color:red;"><b>Idaho</b>, 7.56% </span></li>
<li><span style="color:red;"><b>Utah</b>, 9.64% </span></li>
<li><span style="color:red;"><b>Colorado</b>, 9.73% </span></li></ol>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Electoral_milestones">Electoral milestones</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=1960_United_States_presidential_election&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Electoral milestones">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul><li>This is the only presidential election where no candidate succeeded in carrying every single county or parish in any state.</li>
<li>This was the first election where the winning candidate received a majority of the electoral votes although the second-place candidate carried a majority of the states. As of 2016<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=1960_United_States_presidential_election&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, it has happened once more, in <a href="/wiki/1976_United_States_presidential_election" title="1976 United States presidential election">1976</a>.</li>
<li>Although the vote in both Alaska and Hawaii was very close in 1960, the election would foreshadow their future political leanings: Alaska has continued supporting Republicans in all but one election since (<a href="/wiki/1964_United_States_presidential_election" title="1964 United States presidential election">1964</a>), while Hawaii has been carried by Democrats in all but two subsequent elections (<a href="/wiki/1972_United_States_presidential_election" title="1972 United States presidential election">1972</a> and <a href="/wiki/1984_United_States_presidential_election" title="1984 United States presidential election">1984</a>).</li>
<li>This election was the last time that Georgia, Louisiana, and South Carolina voted Democratic until 1976 and the last time Maine and Washington voted Republican until 1972.</li>
<li>This is the last election when <a href="/wiki/Multnomah_County,_Oregon" title="Multnomah County, Oregon">Multnomah County, Oregon</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Johnson_County,_Iowa" title="Johnson County, Iowa">Johnson County, Iowa</a>, have supported the Republican candidate, but also the last when the <a href="/wiki/North_Florida" title="North Florida">North Florida</a> counties of <a href="/wiki/Escambia_County,_Florida" title="Escambia County, Florida">Escambia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Santa_Rosa_County,_Florida" title="Santa Rosa County, Florida">Santa Rosa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Okaloosa_County,_Florida" title="Okaloosa County, Florida">Okaloosa</a>, along with nearby <a href="/wiki/Harrison_County,_Mississippi" title="Harrison County, Mississippi">Harrison County, Mississippi</a>, have supported a Democrat.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>Kennedy was the fourth winning candidate after <a href="/wiki/Woodrow_Wilson" title="Woodrow Wilson">Woodrow Wilson</a> in <a href="/wiki/1912_United_States_presidential_election" title="1912 United States presidential election">1912</a>, <a href="/wiki/Grover_Cleveland" title="Grover Cleveland">Grover Cleveland</a> in <a href="/wiki/1884_United_States_presidential_election" title="1884 United States presidential election">1884</a> and <a href="/wiki/James_A._Garfield" title="James A. Garfield">James A. Garfield</a> in 1880 to not carry California; since then, <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Carter" title="Jimmy Carter">Jimmy Carter</a> in 1976, <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">George W. Bush</a> in 2000 and 2004, and <a href="/wiki/Donald_Trump" title="Donald Trump">Donald Trump</a> in 2016, won the White House despite losing California.</li>
<li>This was the first election that Arizona participated in which it did not back the winning candidate.</li>
<li>Kennedy was the first Democrat to win the presidency without carrying Arizona, Florida, Oklahoma and Virginia, the last to win without Ohio and Wisconsin, and the first to win without Tennessee and Kentucky since 1852.</li>
<li>Kennedy is the last candidate from either party elected without carrying Ohio, and only <a href="/wiki/Bill_Clinton" title="Bill Clinton">Bill Clinton</a> in <a href="/wiki/1992_United_States_presidential_election" title="1992 United States presidential election">1992</a> has won without Florida since.</li>
<li>Since <a href="/wiki/1840_United_States_presidential_election" title="1840 United States presidential election">1840</a>, this is the second and most recent presidential election in which <a href="/wiki/Alabama" title="Alabama">Alabama</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mississippi" title="Mississippi">Mississippi</a> have had a different popular vote victor. (Mississippi was also unable to vote in <a href="/wiki/1868_United_States_presidential_election" title="1868 United States presidential election">1868</a> due to <a href="/wiki/Reconstruction_Era" class="mw-redirect" title="Reconstruction Era">Reconstruction</a>). However, the majority of Alabama's electoral votes went to <a href="/wiki/Harry_F._Byrd" title="Harry F. Byrd">Harry F. Byrd</a> instead of John F. Kennedy. All of Mississippi's electoral votes went to Byrd.</li>
<li>This election marked the first time since <a href="/wiki/1936_United_States_presidential_election" title="1936 United States presidential election">1936</a> that the Democratic candidate's share of the popular vote went up as opposed to down.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>This was the first election where both major party candidates were born in the 20th century.</li>
<li>It marked the first election of two incumbent U.S. senators as president and vice president, a phenomenon that was repeated by <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a> and <a href="/wiki/Joe_Biden" title="Joe Biden">Joe Biden</a> in <a href="/wiki/2008_United_States_presidential_election" title="2008 United States presidential election">2008</a>. In both instances, the President-elect was the younger of the two and also the <a href="/wiki/Seniority_in_the_United_States_Senate" title="Seniority in the United States Senate">junior senator</a> from his state.</li>
<li>Kennedy was both the last Northern Democrat and sitting United States senator to win either the presidency or popular vote until the election of Barack Obama in 2008.</li>
<li>This was the first presidential election since <a href="/wiki/1860_United_States_presidential_election" title="1860 United States presidential election">1860</a> that the incumbent Vice President was a presidential nominee.</li>
<li>Barry Goldwater was the only person to come in fourth for either President or vice president in the electoral college during the 20th century, and the first since 1872.</li>
<li>As of the 2016 Presidential Election this is the last time that Missouri, Kentucky and Tennessee did not all vote for the same candidate.</li>
<li>This was the only presidential election where both major candidates were less than fifty years of age.</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=1960_United_States_presidential_election&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/History_of_the_United_States_(1945%E2%80%931964)" title="History of the United States (1945–1964)">History of the United States (1945–1964)</a></li>
